Quranic Arguments for 'Ali's Superiority
Sanusi enumerates three Quranic verses used by Shi'a to assert 'Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Quranic verses the Shi'a use to argue for 'Ali's superiority over the other caliphs.

Core Idea
The Shi'a interpret specific Quranic verses as directly referring to 'Ali.

In this passage, the author summarizes the Quranic proof texts that the Shi'a present to support the superiority of 'Ali. These verses include the mubāhala verse, the verse on love for relatives, and the verse on the righteous of the believers. Each is interpreted as pointing to 'Ali's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the main claims. Notice how each verse is cited with a brief interpretive gloss.
2
Identify each verse
List the three verses: the mubāhala verse (3:61), the mawaddah verse (42:23), and the verse on salih al-mu'minin (66:4).
The Shi'a interpret 'ourselves' in 3:61 as 'Ali, and 'the righteous of the believers' as 'Ali.
3
Understand the Shi'a perspective
Ask yourself what these verses are supposed to prove about 'Ali. They are meant to show his elevated status and inclusion in the Prophet's family.
4
Reflect on significance
Why are these verses considered key by the Shi'a? They form the scriptural foundation for the claim of 'Ali's superiority.
Sunni scholars often offer alternative interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : تمسكت الشيعة بقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم ) أراد علياً . وقوله تعالى : (قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربي ). وعلي (رضي الله عنه منهم . وقوله تعالى : (وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين). وهو علي . وبقوله : من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه، وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته، فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب . وقوله : أقضاكم علي. وقوله : اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير، فجاء علي. وقوله : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى . إلى غير ذلك. وبأنه أعلم حتى استند رؤساء العلوم إليه، وأخبر بذلك في خبر الوسادة، وأشهد على ما يشهد به غزواته ، حتى قال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) «الضربة علي خير من عبادة الثقلين وأزهد حتى طلق الدنيا بكليتها، وأكثر عبادة وسخاوة، وأشرف خلقاً وطلاقة، وأفصح لساناً، وأسبق إسلاماً.
He said: The Shi'a adhered to His saying, the Exalted: {Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves} (3:61) meaning 'Ali. And His saying, the Exalted: {Say: I do not ask you for it any reward except love for the relatives} (42:23); and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) is among them. And His saying, the Exalted: {and Gabriel and the righteous of the believers} (66:4); and he is 'Ali. And by his saying: 'Whoever wants to see Adam in his knowledge, and Noah in his piety, and Abraham in his forbearance, and Moses in his awe, and Jesus in his worship, let him look at 'Ali ibn Abi Talib.' And his saying: 'The most knowledgeable among you in judgment is 'Ali.' And his saying: 'O Allah, bring to me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat with me this bird,' and 'Ali came. And his saying: 'You are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses' ... and so on. And because he was the most knowledgeable, to the extent that the leaders of the sciences relied upon him, and he informed of that in the hadith of the pillow, and he witnessed what his battles witness, until the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) said: 'The strike of 'Ali is better than the worship of the two heavy ones (jinn and mankind).' And he was more ascetic, even divorcing this world entirely, and more abundant in worship and generosity, and nobler in character and cheerfulness, and more eloquent in tongue, and foremost in Islam.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage summarizes the arguments that the Shi'a (al-Shīʿa) use to claim the superiority of 'Ali over the other Companions. It begins by citing verses from the Qur'an: the mubāhala verse (Āl 'Imrān 61) where 'the selves' is interpreted as 'Ali; the verse on love for relatives (al-Shūrā 23); and the verse about 'the righteous of the believers' (al-Taḥrīm 4), which they take to refer to 'Ali. Then it lists several hadiths: a composite hadith praising 'Ali as combining the excellences of previous prophets; the hadith 'the most knowledgeable in judgment is 'Ali'; the hadith of the bird; the hadith of the position of Aaron; the hadith that 'Ali's strike is better than worship; and general claims about 'Ali's knowledge, asceticism, worship, generosity, character, eloquence, and precedence in Islam. The passage presents these as the core proof texts of the Shi'a position.

Key Terms
الشيعة (al-Shīʿa)
المباهلة (al-mubāhala)
المودة في القربى (al-mawaddah fī al-qurbā)
صالح المؤمنين (ṣāliḥ al-mu'minīn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These Quranic verses are unambiguous proofs of 'Ali's superiority over all other caliphs.
Correction: Sunni scholars often interpret these verses differently, not as explicit references to 'Ali's superiority.

Checkpoint
Which Qur'anic verse is cited as proof that 'Ali is 'the selves' (anfusanā) of the Prophet?
A) Sūrat al-Shūrā 23
B) Sūrat Āl 'Imrān 61
C) Sūrat al-Taḥrīm 4
D) Sūrat al-Mā'idah 55

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a base a major part of their argument for 'Ali's superiority on specific interpretations of three Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you ever assume that your own sect's interpretation is the only correct one, without considering that sincere believers from other schools understand the same verses differently? What would it cost you to honestly examine an interpretation that challenges your tradition?
?
These Shia proof texts are deeply cherished by them. Can you think of a verse or teaching in your own tradition that you hold dear, even if others interpret it differently? Does that awareness humble you or harden you?
?
If you were in a debate about 'Ali's superiority and someone cited these verses, would you be able to respond with knowledge rather than dismissal? What would it take for you to actually study the arguments of those you disagree with, not to win, but to understand?

Prophetic Hadiths and Other Evidences for 'Ali's Virtue
Sanusi lists several prophetic hadiths and claims used by Shi'a to prove 'Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with the prophetic hadiths and other claims the Shi'a use to argue for 'Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Prophetic traditions are cited to show 'Ali's unique virtues and his status relative to the Prophet.

After Quranic verses, the Shi'a also rely on several well-known hadiths that highlight 'Ali's exceptional qualities. These traditions cover his knowledge, his role as the Prophet's beloved, and his status akin to Aaron. Additional claims about his asceticism and precedence round out the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to note each hadith and claim.
2
Identify key hadiths
List the hadiths: the composite hadith of prophets' excellences, 'most knowledgeable in judgment', the hadith of the bird, the hadith of the position of Aaron, and the hadith of the strike.
The 'hadith of the pillow' is mentioned but not clear.
3
Analyze the themes
The hadiths emphasize 'Ali's comprehensive virtues: knowledge, rank, asceticism, and precedence in Islam.
4
Consider cumulative force
Reflect on how these multiple proofs are intended to establish 'Ali's superiority beyond doubt.
Sunni scholars question the authenticity or interpretation of some of these hadiths.

Redefining Superiority: The Sunni Rebuttal
The author refutes the Shi'a proofs and presents counter-evidence from Sunni sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni response to Shi'a arguments for 'Ali's superiority reframes the discussion around the true meaning of afḍaliyya as greater honor and reward with Allah.

Core Idea
Superiority means greater honor and reward with Allah, not worldly status.

After presenting the Shi'a proofs, the author now delivers the Sunni rebuttal. He clarifies that the debate is about nearness to Allah, not mere worldly merit. He appeals to the consensus of Muslims and even 'Ali's own acknowledgment. This passage sets the foundation for a deeper comparison of the Companions' virtues.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's concise style come through.
Notice that the passage is a direct response to previous Shi'a arguments.
2
Identify the definition of afḍaliyya
The author defines superiority as honor (karāmah) with Allah and abundance of reward (thawāb). This is the key framing.
This redefinition is the core of the Sunni rebuttal.
3
Notice the sources of evidence
The author cites the general consensus of Muslims and 'Ali's own testimony. This grounds the response in accepted authority.
Think about why citing 'Ali himself is rhetorically powerful.
4
Reflect on the hinted counter-evidence
The passage ends by referencing 'precedence in Islam and the sword in raising high the banners.' This alludes to the virtues of Abu Bakr and 'Umar.
This hint will be unpacked in subsequent lessons.
5
Consider the overall argument
Ask yourself: How does redefining superiority change the debate? Why is the focus on honor with Allah important?
This connects to the broader Sunni doctrine of the Companions' ranking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أن الكلام في الأفضلية بمعنى الكرامة عند الله، وكثرة الثواب، وقد شهد في ذلك عامة المسلمين، واعترف علي رضي الله عنه به . وعارض ما ذكرتم ما ذكرنا مع أن فيه مواضع بحث لا تخفى، سيما حديث سبق الإسلام والسيف في إعلاء الأعلام.
The answer is that the discussion about superiority is in the sense of honor (karāmah) with Allah and abundance of reward (thawāb). The general body of Muslims have testified to that, and 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) acknowledged it. What you have mentioned is countered by what we have mentioned, although in it are points of investigation not hidden, especially the hadith of precedence in Islam and the sword in raising high the banners.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage gives the Sunni response to the Shi'a proofs for 'Ali's superiority. It begins by clarifying the nature of afḍaliyya: it means greater honor and reward with Allah, not merely worldly status. The response states that the consensus (or overwhelming majority) of Muslims has attested to the order of virtue (Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then 'Uthmān), and that 'Ali himself acknowledged this (as shown in the earlier reports). The author then claims that the Shi'a proofs are countered by Sunni evidences, and he hints at several points of critical discussion, especially the hadith about 'precedence in Islam and the sword in raising high the banners'—likely a reference to the virtues of Abu Bakr's early conversion and 'Umar's role in spreading Islam by force. This sets the stage for a detailed comparison.

Key Terms
أفضلية (afḍaliyya)
كرامة (karāmah)
ثواب (thawāb)
سبق الإسلام (sabaq al-Islām)
السيف (al-sayf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Superiority means being better in worldly conquests, political leadership, or personal achievements.
Correction: The text explicitly states that superiority (afḍaliyya) is about honor with Allah and abundance of reward, not worldly criteria.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response to Shi'a arguments for 'Ali's superiority centers on redefining superiority as nearness to Allah, citing consensus and specific hadiths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage defines true superiority as honor and reward with Allah. If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow, would your deeds reflect that you genuinely sought His honor over the approval of people? What is one thing you can change today to align your actions with this truth?

The Quranic Proof for 'Ali's Superiority: The Mubāhala Verse
Enumerates three sources and begins Quranic quotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents of 'Ali's superiority begin their case by citing the mubāhala verse as a Quranic proof, and the significance of the word 'ourselves' in that argument.

Core Idea
The mubāhala verse is a key Quranic text used to argue for 'Ali's superiority.

This lesson introduces the arguments of those who maintain that 'Ali (ra) is superior to the other Companions. They rely on three sources: the Qur'an, the Sunna, and reason. The Qur'anic proof they cite is the mubāhala verse (Āl 'Imrān 61), which they interpret as including 'Ali among 'ourselves' (anfusanā), indicating his high rank. The passage here sets up their position, though the full argument continues beyond this page.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and literal translation to get the basic information about the proponents' sources.
The passage is incomplete; the full argument continues.
2
Identify the three sources
Notice that the proponents are said to rely on the Book, Sunna, and reason; the Book is mentioned first.
3
Focus on the Quranic proof
The verse quoted is the mubāhala verse; note the phrase 'ourselves' which they interpret as referring to 'Ali.
4
Understand the incomplete nature
Recognize that the passage cuts off; the full argument would require more text from the next page.
This lesson only covers the opening of the Quranic proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأفضلية علي (رضي الله عنه) تمسكوا بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم . . )(٥) الآية .
Those who hold 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) as superior adhered to the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book, His saying, the Exalted: {Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves} (3:61) ... the verse.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This short passage introduces a new section presenting the arguments of those who maintain 'Ali's superiority. It groups them as 'those who hold 'Ali superior' (al-qā'ilūn bi-afḍaliyyat 'Alī). They rely on three sources: the Qur'an, the Sunna, and rational proofs. The text then begins to quote the Qur'anic proof, citing the mubāhala verse (Āl 'Imrān 61) once again, emphasizing the inclusion of 'ourselves' (anfusanā) as referring to 'Ali. The passage is incomplete due to the page ending, but it establishes the pattern for their argumentation. This lesson captures the opening of their case from the Qur'an.

الكتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this is a separate group from the Shi'a, but it refers to the same position; the author distinguishes between the Shi'a and those who argue from the same texts.
Correction: The proponents here are not a distinct group but rather the same position that argues for 'Ali's superiority; the broader context of the text differentiates them from the Shi'a.

Checkpoint
Which source is mentioned first in the argument of those who hold 'Ali superior?
A) The Sunna
B) Reason (al-ma'qūl)
C) The Book (al-kitāb)
D) Consensus (al-ijmā')

Key Takeaway
The proponents of 'Ali's superiority begin their case with the Qur'an, specifically the mubāhala verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proponents of 'Ali's superiority began with the Qur'an. When you defend your beliefs about the Ahl al-Bayt, do you start with the Qur'an as your foundation? If not, what do you start with, and why?
?
This passage mentions three sources: the Book, the Sunna, and reason. Which of these do you personally struggle to engage with most? What would it cost you to give that source its proper weight in your faith?
?
The mubāhala verse includes 'ourselves'—interpreted by some as 'Ali. How does this interpretation confront your current understanding of 'Ali's status? Are you willing to let the Qur'an challenge your preconceptions?

The Mubahala Proof for Ali's Superiority
Presents and explains the mubahala verse as proof for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse of mubahala is used to argue for Ali's superiority over other Companions.

Core Idea
The Prophet included Ali as 'himself' in the mubahala, implying his unique status.

The first Quranic proof for Ali's superiority comes from the mubahala verse (3:61). The author explains how the Prophet's inclusion of Ali in 'ourselves' indicates his exceptional rank. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the mubahala proof passage
Read the Arabic text and translation of the section discussing Surah Al-Imran 3:61. Notice how the author interprets 'ourselves' as referring to Ali.
The verse is about invoking a curse with the Christians of Najran.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that since the Prophet only brought Ali from his male cousins, and called him 'our selves', Ali must be like the Prophet's own self, hence superior.
3
Reflect on the context
Remember that this argument is presented as part of a larger discussion; the author will later refute it. This lesson only covers the claim.
The passage is from a Sunni theological work, not an endorsement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأفضلية علي (رضي الله عنه) تمسكوا بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم . . )(٥) الآية . عن بأنفسنا علياً (رضي الله تعالى عنه وإن كان صيغة جمع ، لأنه دعا وفد الله نجران إلى المباهلة وهو الدعاء على الظالم من الفريقين خرج ومعه الحسن والحسين وفاطمة وعلي، وهو يقول لهم : إذا أنا دعوت فأمنوا، ولم يخرج معه من بني عمه غير علي رضي الله عنه ولا شك أن من كان بمنزلة نفس النبي الا لو كان أفضل . (١) وقوله تعالى : ( قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربى (١) قال سعيد بن جبير لما نزلت هذه الآية قالوا يا رسول الله ، من هؤلاء الذين تودّهم؟ قال علي وفاطمة وولداها ولا يخفى أن من وجبت محبته : بحكم نص الكتاب كان أفضل. وكذا من ثبت نصرته للرسول بالعطف في كلام الله تعالى عنه على اسم الله وجبريل مع التعبير عنه بصالح المؤمنين، وذلك قوله تعالى : فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين (۲) . فعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه أن المراد به علي . وأما السنة، فقوله (عليه السلام) من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ولا خفاء في أن من ساوى هؤلاء الأنبياء في هذه الكمالات كان أفضل . وقوله : «أقضاكم علي » (۳) والأقضى أكمل وأعلم. وقوله ( ) : «اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير» فجاءه علي فأكل معه. والأحب إلى الله أكثر ثواباً، وهو معنى من الأفضل، وبقوله ( عليه السلام) : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى (٤) ولم يكن عند موسى أفضل من هارون وقوله عليه السلام): «من کنت مولاه فعلي مولاه» (٥) الحديث، وقوله ( ) يوم خيبر : لأعطين هذه الراية غداً رجلاً
Those who affirm the superiority of Ali (may God be pleased with him) adhered to the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book, His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves'... the verse. He meant by 'ourselves' Ali (may God the Exalted be pleased with him), even though it is a plural form, because he called the delegation of Najran to the mubahala, which is invoking curse upon the wrongdoer from the two parties. He went out with al-Hasan, al-Husayn, Fatima, and Ali, and he said to them: 'When I pray, say amen.' And none of his male cousins went out with him except Ali (may God be pleased with him). There is no doubt that whoever is in the position of the self of the Prophet would only be superior. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: I do not ask you for it any recompense except love of the relatives.' Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: When this verse was revealed, they said: O Messenger of God, who are those whom we love? He said: Ali, Fatima, and their two sons. And it is not hidden that whoever's love is made obligatory by the text of the Book is superior. Also, whoever's support for the Messenger is established by inclusion alongside the name of God and Gabriel in God's speech, expressed as 'righteous believers'—that is His saying, the Exalted: 'For God is his patron, and Gabriel, and the righteous believers.' It is reported from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that the intended one is Ali. As for the Sunna, his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever wishes to look at Adam in his knowledge, at Noah in his piety, at Abraham in his forbearance, at Moses in his majesty, and at Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ali ibn Abi Talib.' There is no doubt that whoever equals these prophets in these perfections is superior. And his saying: 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' And 'the most judicious' is more complete and more knowledgeable. And his saying: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird with me.' So Ali came to him and ate with him. The most beloved to God is greater in reward, and that is the meaning of superiority. And by his saying (peace be upon him): 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' and there was none better than Aaron in Moses' time. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla'—the hadith. And his saying on the day of Khaybar: 'Tomorrow I will give this banner to a man...'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who claim the superiority (afdaliyya) of Ali ibn Abi Talib over other Companions, drawing on the Quran and Sunna. It is structured as a list of proofs. First, the Quranic verse from Surah Al 'Imran (3:61) about the mubahala (invoking curse) with the Christians of Najran. The author explains that the Prophet used 'ourselves' (anfusana) including Ali, and only Ali from his male cousins accompanied him, implying Ali is like the Prophet's own self, hence superior. Second, the verse from Surah Ash-Shura (42:23) commanding love of relatives (al-qurba); Saeed ibn Jubayr interprets the relatives as Ali, Fatima, and their sons, and such obligatory love indicates superiority. Third, the verse from Surah At-Tahrim (66:4) where God, Gabriel, and the 'righteous believers' are mentioned; Ibn Abbas identifies the righteous believers as Ali, so his support for the Prophet is paired with divine aid, showing his status. Then the passage moves to hadiths: (1) The Prophet likening Ali to Adam in knowledge, Noah in piety, Abraham in forbearance, Moses in majesty, and Jesus in worship—whoever equals these prophets is clearly superior. (2) The saying 'The most judicious among you is Ali' (aqdakum Ali) implying completeness and knowledge. (3) The prayer that God bring the most beloved creation to eat with him, and Ali comes. (4) The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' indicating Ali's closeness and status, as Aaron was Moses' brother and supporter. (5) The hadith 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla' (from Ghadir Khumm) implying leadership. (6) The promise to give the banner at Khaybar to a man (who turned out to be Ali). The passage ends mid-sentence on page 296.

Key Terms
المباهلة (mubahala)
أفضلية (superiority)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mubahala verse definitively proves Ali's superiority over all Companions.
Correction: In the context of Sharh al-Maqasid, this argument is presented to be refuted later; the author does not endorse it as conclusive.

Checkpoint
Which event is the basis for the argument that Ali is like the Prophet's 'self'?
A) The Battle of Badr
B) The mubahala with Christians of Najran
C) The conquest of Khaybar
D) The incident of Ghadir Khumm

Key Takeaway
The mubahala verse is used to argue that Ali holds a status equivalent to the Prophet's own self, implying superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Ali is truly in the position of the Prophet's own self, then his example carries prophetic weight. Are you honestly following Ali's example in your daily life? Name one area where you fall short and commit to change.
?
The mubahala involved invoking a curse on the wrongdoer. If you were engaged in a religious dispute, would you be willing to call upon Allah to curse you if you were wrong? What does your hesitation reveal about your certainty in your own beliefs?
?
This lesson interprets a Quranic verse in a way that challenges common assumptions. How do you ensure your own interpretations of the Quran are not shaped by sect or desire rather than truth? What concrete step will you take this week to guard against bias?

The Love of Relatives Proof
Uses Quran 42:23 and its interpretation to argue for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic command to love the relatives (qurba) is used to argue for Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Obligatory love for Ali and his family implies their superiority over others.

The second Quranic proof is based on the verse 'Say: I do not ask you for any recompense except love of the relatives' (42:23). The author cites an interpretation that identifies these relatives as Ali, Fatima, and their sons. Let's see how this leads to the claim of superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and interpretation
Read the Arabic text and translation for the section on Surah Ash-Shura 42:23. Note the explanation by Sa'id ibn Jubayr identifying the relatives.
2
Analyze the reasoning
The author argues that if love for these individuals is made obligatory by the Quran, then they must be superior, as obligatory love implies high status.
3
Consider the context
Again, remember that this is a presentation of an argument that will be examined critically later.

The Righteous Believers Proof
Uses Quran 66:4 interpreted as referring to Ali to argue for his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic phrase 'righteous believers' is interpreted to support Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Ali is named alongside God and Gabriel as a supporter of the Prophet, indicating his high rank.

The third Quranic proof comes from Surah At-Tahrim (66:4), where God, Gabriel, and the 'righteous believers' are mentioned as supporters of the Prophet. Ibn 'Abbas identifies the 'righteous believers' as Ali, thereby linking him directly with divine support. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and interpretation
Read the Arabic text and translation for the section on Surah At-Tahrim 66:4. Note the interpretation by Ibn 'Abbas.
2
Understand the argument
The author argues that being mentioned alongside God and Gabriel in support of the Prophet shows Ali's supreme status, implying he is the best of believers.
3
Reflect on the context
This is another argument that will be critiqued later in the larger discussion.

Also, whoever's support for the Messenger is established by inclusion alongside the name of God and Gabriel in God's speech, expressed as 'righteous believers'—that is His saying, the Exalted: 'For God is his patron, and Gabriel, and the righteous believers.' It is reported from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that the intended one is Ali. As for the Sunna, his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever wishes to look at Adam in his knowledge, at Noah in his piety, at Abraham in his forbearance, at Moses in his majesty, and at Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ali ibn Abi Talib.' There is no doubt that whoever equals these prophets in these perfections is superior. And his saying: 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' And 'the most judicious' is more complete and more knowledgeable. And his saying: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird with me.' So Ali came to him and ate with him. The most beloved to God is greater in reward, and that is the meaning of superiority. And by his saying (peace be upon him): 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' and there was none better than Aaron in Moses' time. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla'—the hadith. And his saying on the day of Khaybar: 'Tomorrow I will give this banner to a man...'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who claim the superiority (afdaliyya) of Ali ibn Abi Talib over other Companions, drawing on the Quran and Sunna. It is structured as a list of proofs. First, the Quranic verse from Surah Al 'Imran (3:61) about the mubahala (invoking curse) with the Christians of Najran. The author explains that the Prophet used 'ourselves' (anfusana) including Ali, and only Ali from his male cousins accompanied him, implying Ali is like the Prophet's own self, hence superior. Second, the verse from Surah Ash-Shura (42:23) commanding love of relatives (al-qurba); Saeed ibn Jubayr interprets the relatives as Ali, Fatima, and their sons, and such obligatory love indicates superiority. Third, the verse from Surah At-Tahrim (66:4) where God, Gabriel, and the 'righteous believers' are mentioned; Ibn Abbas identifies the righteous believers as Ali, so his support for the Prophet is paired with divine aid, showing his status. Then the passage moves to hadiths: (1) The Prophet likening Ali to Adam in knowledge, Noah in piety, Abraham in forbearance, Moses in majesty, and Jesus in worship—whoever equals these prophets is clearly superior. (2) The saying 'The most judicious among you is Ali' (aqdakum Ali) implying completeness and knowledge. (3) The prayer that God bring the most beloved creation to eat with him, and Ali comes. (4) The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' indicating Ali's closeness and status, as Aaron was Moses' brother and supporter. (5) The hadith 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla' (from Ghadir Khumm) implying leadership. (6) The promise to give the banner at Khaybar to a man (who turned out to be Ali). The passage ends mid-sentence on page 296.

Key Terms
صالح المؤمنين (righteous believers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'righteous believers' in 66:4 exclusively refers to Ali at the time of revelation.
Correction: Other interpretations exist; the verse is general and the specific identification with Ali is one interpretation.

Checkpoint
Who is said to be the intended meaning of 'righteous believers' in Quran 66:4 according to Ibn 'Abbas?
A) Abu Bakr
B) Umar
C) Ali
D) All believers

Key Takeaway
Ali's identification as the 'righteous believer' alongside God and Gabriel is used to argue for his supreme status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If being mentioned alongside Allah and Jibril is the ultimate honor, who are the 'righteous believers' in your life? Are you counted among them? What deficiency in your faith prevents you from being one? Pledge one deed today that aligns you with Allah's supporters.
?
The Prophet had Allah, Jibril, and Ali as supporters. Who are your supporters in your spiritual journey? Are they drawing you closer to Allah or away? Commit to seeking the company of the righteous from this day forward.
?
Ibn Abbas identified Ali as the 'righteous believer' — the one who truly supports the Prophet. If your actions were judged, would you be identified as a supporter of the Prophet's message? What specific change can you make this week to earn that title?

Hadith Proofs: Equality with Prophets and Aqdakum
Presents two hadiths as evidence for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two hadiths—comparing Ali to prophets and calling him 'most judicious'—are used to support his superiority.

Core Idea
If Ali equals prophets in perfections, he is superior; being 'most judicious' implies completeness.

The first two hadiths presented are: (1) The Prophet said whoever wants to see the virtues of various prophets should look at Ali, and (2) 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' These are used to claim that Ali combines the perfections of multiple prophets and possesses superior judgment. Let's examine them.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths
Read the Arabic and translation for the two hadiths: the one comparing Ali to Adam, Noah, Abraham, Moses, and Jesus, and the 'aqdakum' hadith.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that resemblance to prophets in their distinctive qualities indicates superiority, and being 'most judicious' (aqdak) implies superior knowledge and completeness.
3
Reflect on the chain
These arguments are presented as part of a broader discussion; later the author will evaluate their strength.

Source Text

القائلون بأفضلية علي (رضي الله عنه) تمسكوا بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم . . )(٥) الآية . عن بأنفسنا علياً (رضي الله تعالى عنه وإن كان صيغة جمع ، لأنه دعا وفد الله نجران إلى المباهلة وهو الدعاء على الظالم من الفريقين خرج ومعه الحسن والحسين وفاطمة وعلي، وهو يقول لهم : إذا أنا دعوت فأمنوا، ولم يخرج معه من بني عمه غير علي رضي الله عنه ولا شك أن من كان بمنزلة نفس النبي الا لو كان أفضل . (١) وقوله تعالى : ( قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربى (١) قال سعيد بن جبير لما نزلت هذه الآية قالوا يا رسول الله ، من هؤلاء الذين تودّهم؟ قال علي وفاطمة وولداها ولا يخفى أن من وجبت محبته : بحكم نص الكتاب كان أفضل. وكذا من ثبت نصرته للرسول بالعطف في كلام الله تعالى عنه على اسم الله وجبريل مع التعبير عنه بصالح المؤمنين، وذلك قوله تعالى : فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين (۲) . فعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه أن المراد به علي . وأما السنة، فقوله (عليه السلام) من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ولا خفاء في أن من ساوى هؤلاء الأنبياء في هذه الكمالات كان أفضل . وقوله : «أقضاكم علي » (۳) والأقضى أكمل وأعلم. وقوله ( ) : «اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير» فجاءه علي فأكل معه. والأحب إلى الله أكثر ثواباً، وهو معنى من الأفضل، وبقوله ( عليه السلام) : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى (٤) ولم يكن عند موسى أفضل من هارون وقوله عليه السلام): «من کنت مولاه فعلي مولاه» (٥) الحديث، وقوله ( ) يوم خيبر : لأعطين هذه الراية غداً رجلاً
Those who affirm the superiority of Ali (may God be pleased with him) adhered to the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book, His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves'... the verse. He meant by 'ourselves' Ali (may God the Exalted be pleased with him), even though it is a plural form, because he called the delegation of Najran to the mubahala, which is invoking curse upon the wrongdoer from the two parties. He went out with al-Hasan, al-Husayn, Fatima, and Ali, and he said to them: 'When I pray, say amen.' And none of his male cousins went out with him except Ali (may God be pleased with him). There is no doubt that whoever is in the position of the self of the Prophet would only be superior. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: I do not ask you for it any recompense except love of the relatives.' Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: When this verse was revealed, they said: O Messenger of God, who are those whom we love? He said: Ali, Fatima, and their two sons. And it is not hidden that whoever's love is made obligatory by the text of the Book is superior. Also, whoever's support for the Messenger is established by inclusion alongside the name of God and Gabriel in God's speech, expressed as 'righteous believers'—that is His saying, the Exalted: 'For God is his patron, and Gabriel, and the righteous believers.' It is reported from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that the intended one is Ali. As for the Sunna, his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever wishes to look at Adam in his knowledge, at Noah in his piety, at Abraham in his forbearance, at Moses in his majesty, and at Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ali ibn Abi Talib.' There is no doubt that whoever equals these prophets in these perfections is superior. And his saying: 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' And 'the most judicious' is more complete and more knowledgeable. And his saying: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird with me.' So Ali came to him and ate with him. The most beloved to God is greater in reward, and that is the meaning of superiority. And by his saying (peace be upon him): 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' and there was none better than Aaron in Moses' time. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla'—the hadith. And his saying on the day of Khaybar: 'Tomorrow I will give this banner to a man...'

?

?
The hadith calls Ali the 'most judicious'—a wisdom rooted in completeness and knowledge. Think of a recent decision you made. Was it driven by haste or worldly concerns? How might you cultivate the same depth of judgment in your choices from now on?
?
If Ali embodies the best of each prophet, then our own pursuit of perfection is not about achieving greatness for its own sake, but about closeness to Allah. What is the one character flaw you have been excusing that prevents you from drawing closer to Him? Will you commit today to uprooting it?

Hadith Proofs: Most Beloved and Harun Comparison
Presents two hadiths about Ali's status as evidence for his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadiths about Ali being the most beloved to God and his being like Aaron to Moses are used to argue for his superiority.

Core Idea
Being most beloved to God implies greater reward and superiority; Aaron's status with Moses shows Ali's high rank.

The next two hadiths in the list are: (1) The Prophet prayed for the most beloved creation to eat with him, and Ali came; (2) The Prophet said to Ali, 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses.' Both are used to demonstrate Ali's special closeness to God and the Prophet, implying his superiority over all others.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths
Read the Arabic and translation for the two hadiths: the 'most beloved' hadith and the 'Harun' hadith.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that being the most beloved to God entails greater reward and thus superiority. The Harun hadith is used to show that Ali is to the Prophet as Aaron was to Moses – there was no one better than Aaron in Moses' time.
3
Note the logic
The author explicitly says 'the most beloved to God is greater in reward, which is the meaning of superiority.' For the Harun hadith, similarly, no one was better than Aaron, so Ali is best.

Hadith Proofs: Mawla and Khaybar
Presents the 'Mawla' hadith and begins the Khaybar hadith as proofs for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the 'Mawla' hadith (Ghadir Khumm) and the Khaybar banner hadith are used to argue for Ali's superiority, even though the passage is incomplete.

Core Idea
Being declared mawla of believers and given the banner at Khaybar imply leadership and hence superiority.

The final two hadiths in this passage are the famous 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa Aliun mawlahu' and the promise to give the banner at Khaybar to a man (later identified as Ali). These are often cited to show Ali's authority. However, the passage ends mid-sentence with the Khaybar hadith. Let's examine what is available.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith fragments
Read the Arabic and translation for the 'Mawla' hadith and the start of the Khaybar hadith. Note that the Khaybar text is cut off.
2
Understand the argument
The 'Mawla' hadith is used by some to claim Ali is the leader of believers; the Khaybar hadith is used to show he was chosen for a special mission, indicating his merit.
3
Note the incomplete ending
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full Khaybar story is not provided. In the overall text, this argument would be completed.

Source Text

القائلون بأفضلية علي (رضي الله عنه) تمسكوا بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول. أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى قل تعالوا ندع أبناءنا وأبناءكم ونساءنا ونساءكم وأنفسنا وأنفسكم . . )(٥) الآية . عن بأنفسنا علياً (رضي الله تعالى عنه وإن كان صيغة جمع ، لأنه دعا وفد الله نجران إلى المباهلة وهو الدعاء على الظالم من الفريقين خرج ومعه الحسن والحسين وفاطمة وعلي، وهو يقول لهم : إذا أنا دعوت فأمنوا، ولم يخرج معه من بني عمه غير علي رضي الله عنه ولا شك أن من كان بمنزلة نفس النبي الا لو كان أفضل . (١) وقوله تعالى : ( قل لا أسألكم عليه أجراً إلا المودة في القربى (١) قال سعيد بن جبير لما نزلت هذه الآية قالوا يا رسول الله ، من هؤلاء الذين تودّهم؟ قال علي وفاطمة وولداها ولا يخفى أن من وجبت محبته : بحكم نص الكتاب كان أفضل. وكذا من ثبت نصرته للرسول بالعطف في كلام الله تعالى عنه على اسم الله وجبريل مع التعبير عنه بصالح المؤمنين، وذلك قوله تعالى : فإن الله هو مولاه وجبريل وصالح المؤمنين (۲) . فعن ابن عباس رضي الله عنه أن المراد به علي . وأما السنة، فقوله (عليه السلام) من أراد أن ينظر إلى آدم في علمه، وإلى نوح في تقواه وإلى إبراهيم في حلمه، وإلى موسى في هيبته، وإلى عيسى في عبادته فلينظر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ولا خفاء في أن من ساوى هؤلاء الأنبياء في هذه الكمالات كان أفضل . وقوله : «أقضاكم علي » (۳) والأقضى أكمل وأعلم. وقوله ( ) : «اللهم ائتني بأحب خلقك إليك يأكل معي هذا الطير» فجاءه علي فأكل معه. والأحب إلى الله أكثر ثواباً، وهو معنى من الأفضل، وبقوله ( عليه السلام) : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى (٤) ولم يكن عند موسى أفضل من هارون وقوله عليه السلام): «من کنت مولاه فعلي مولاه» (٥) الحديث، وقوله ( ) يوم خيبر : لأعطين هذه الراية غداً رجلاً
Those who affirm the superiority of Ali (may God be pleased with him) adhered to the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book, His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves'... the verse. He meant by 'ourselves' Ali (may God the Exalted be pleased with him), even though it is a plural form, because he called the delegation of Najran to the mubahala, which is invoking curse upon the wrongdoer from the two parties. He went out with al-Hasan, al-Husayn, Fatima, and Ali, and he said to them: 'When I pray, say amen.' And none of his male cousins went out with him except Ali (may God be pleased with him). There is no doubt that whoever is in the position of the self of the Prophet would only be superior. And His saying, the Exalted: 'Say: I do not ask you for it any recompense except love of the relatives.' Sa'id ibn Jubayr said: When this verse was revealed, they said: O Messenger of God, who are those whom we love? He said: Ali, Fatima, and their two sons. And it is not hidden that whoever's love is made obligatory by the text of the Book is superior. Also, whoever's support for the Messenger is established by inclusion alongside the name of God and Gabriel in God's speech, expressed as 'righteous believers'—that is His saying, the Exalted: 'For God is his patron, and Gabriel, and the righteous believers.' It is reported from Ibn 'Abbas (may God be pleased with him) that the intended one is Ali. As for the Sunna, his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever wishes to look at Adam in his knowledge, at Noah in his piety, at Abraham in his forbearance, at Moses in his majesty, and at Jesus in his worship, let him look at Ali ibn Abi Talib.' There is no doubt that whoever equals these prophets in these perfections is superior. And his saying: 'The most judicious among you is Ali.' And 'the most judicious' is more complete and more knowledgeable. And his saying: 'O God, bring me the most beloved of Your creation to You to eat this bird with me.' So Ali came to him and ate with him. The most beloved to God is greater in reward, and that is the meaning of superiority. And by his saying (peace be upon him): 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses,' and there was none better than Aaron in Moses' time. And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla'—the hadith. And his saying on the day of Khaybar: 'Tomorrow I will give this banner to a man...'
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who claim the superiority (afdaliyya) of Ali ibn Abi Talib over other Companions, drawing on the Quran and Sunna. It is structured as a list of proofs. First, the Quranic verse from Surah Al 'Imran (3:61) about the mubahala (invoking curse) with the Christians of Najran. The author explains that the Prophet used 'ourselves' (anfusana) including Ali, and only Ali from his male cousins accompanied him, implying Ali is like the Prophet's own self, hence superior. Second, the verse from Surah Ash-Shura (42:23) commanding love of relatives (al-qurba); Saeed ibn Jubayr interprets the relatives as Ali, Fatima, and their sons, and such obligatory love indicates superiority. Third, the verse from Surah At-Tahrim (66:4) where God, Gabriel, and the 'righteous believers' are mentioned; Ibn Abbas identifies the righteous believers as Ali, so his support for the Prophet is paired with divine aid, showing his status. Then the passage moves to hadiths: (1) The Prophet likening Ali to Adam in knowledge, Noah in piety, Abraham in forbearance, Moses in majesty, and Jesus in worship—whoever equals these prophets is clearly superior. (2) The saying 'The most judicious among you is Ali' (aqdakum Ali) implying completeness and knowledge. (3) The prayer that God bring the most beloved creation to eat with him, and Ali comes. (4) The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' indicating Ali's closeness and status, as Aaron was Moses' brother and supporter. (5) The hadith 'Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla' (from Ghadir Khumm) implying leadership. (6) The promise to give the banner at Khaybar to a man (who turned out to be Ali). The passage ends mid-sentence on page 296.

Key Terms
المولى (mawla)
خيبر (Khaybar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'Mawla' hadith definitively establishes Ali's superiority and right to leadership.
Correction: The interpretation of 'mawla' is disputed; it may simply mean 'friend' rather than 'leader'. The author will later discuss these interpretations.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet say in the hadith that begins 'من کنت مولاه فعلي مولاه'?
A) Ali is the best of companions
B) Whoever's mawla I am, Ali is his mawla
C) Ali will be the caliph after me
D) Ali is my brother

Key Takeaway
These hadiths are used to argue that Ali holds a special position of authority and is chosen by the Prophet, implying superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet declared that whoever accepts him as mawla must also accept Ali as mawla. Who in your life has been placed over you by Allah and His Messenger? Are you truly loyal to them, or is there resistance in your heart? What one step will you take today to align your loyalty with theirs?
?
Prophet Muhammad singled out one man for the banner at Khaybar. Do you believe that Allah has singled you out for a specific mission? What excuses have you been making to avoid stepping into it? Write down one concrete task you will undertake this week to fulfill that role.
?
The term 'mawla' implies a deep bond that transforms allegiance. Is your relationship with the Prophet merely a mental agreement, or does it actively shape whom you trust and follow? Identify one person or group you have distanced yourself from due to pride or ignorance. What will you do to repair that relationship in obedience to this hadith?

Sects and Disagreements

Merits Do Not Prove Absolute Superiority
The author responds that Ali's merits do not establish his superiority over Abu Bakr and Umar, due to consensus and interpretive nuances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a person's many virtues do not automatically establish their absolute superiority (afdaliyya) over others, because consensus (ijma) and other proofs can outweigh personal merits.

Core Idea
Virtues alone do not determine rank; consensus and interpretation of evidence matter.

In this passage, the author addresses a common argument used in sectarian debates: that Ali's numerous merits prove he is superior to Abu Bakr and Umar. He acknowledges all of Ali's virtues but then shows why they do not lead to the conclusion of absolute superiority. This response introduces a key principle in Islamic theology: personal merits must be weighed against other forms of evidence, especially consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author first acknowledges Ali's merits, then gives the main objection.
2
Identify the main claim and response
Identify the claim that Ali's merits imply his superiority. Then see how the author responds: he does not deny the merits but denies the conclusion, citing consensus and Ali's own acknowledgment.
The phrase 'ثبت من الاتفاق الجاري مجرى الإجماع' is key.
3
Examine the interpretive nuances
The author lists several 'points of investigation' (مواضع بحث) that undercut the arguments from specific merits. Find each one and note how it reinterprets the evidence.
There are seven points; they start with 'على أن فيما ذكر مواضع بحث'.
4
Understand the role of consensus
Reflect on why consensus is given such weight. In Sunni theology, ijma is a primary source of authority, so here it outweighs individual merits.
This connects to the learning objective.
5
Apply the principle
Think about how this principle applies beyond this debate. Should we always consider other evidence before concluding someone's rank based on their virtues?
This prepares you for similar arguments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أنه لا كلام في عموم مناقبه ووفور فضائله، واتصافه بالكمالات، و اختصاصه بالكرامات ، إلا أنه لا يدل على الأفضلية بمعنى زيادة الثواب والكرامة عند الله بعدما ثبت من الاتفاق الجاري مجرى الإجماع على أفضلية أبي بكر، ثم عمر . والاعتراف من علي بذلك. على أن فيما ذكر مواضع بحث لا تخفى على المحصل مثل : أن المراد بأنفسنا نفس النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) كما يقال : دعوت نفسي إلى كذا وأن وجوب المحبة وثبوت النصرة على تقدير تحققه في حق علي (رضي الله عنه) فلا اختصاص به وكذا الكمالات الثابتة للمذكورين من الأنبياء، وأن ( أحب خلقك» يحتمل تخصيص أبي بكر وعمر منه، عملاً بأدلة أفضليتهما، ويحتمل يراد ( أحب الخلق إليك» في أن يأكل منه . وأن حكم الأخوة ثابت في حق أبي بكر وعثمان (رضي الله عنهما) أيضاً حيث قال في حق أبي بكر: لكنه أخي وصاحبي ووزيري وقال في عثمان : أخي ورفيقي في الجنة. وأما حديث العلم والشجاعة، فلم تقع حادثة إلا ولأبي بكر وعمر فيه رأي، وعند الاختلاف لم يكن يرجع إلى قول علي رضي الله تعالى عنه البتة بل قد وقد . ولم يكن رباط الجأش وشجاعة القلب وترك الاكتراث في المهالك في أبي بكر أقل من أحد، سيما فيما وقع بعد النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) من حوادث يكاد يصيب وهنا في الإسلام. وليس الخير في هداية من الخزيرة، فلم تم نضجه أتى مسكين فأخرجوا إليه الطعام، ثم عمل الثلث الثاني، فلما تم نضجه أتى يتيم فسأل فأطعموه، ثم عمل الثلث الباقي، فلما تم إنضاجه أتى أسير من المشركين فأطعموه ، وطووا ذلك، فأنزلت فيه هذه الآيات.
And the answer: There is no argument about the generality of his merits and the abundance of his virtues, his possession of perfections, and his exclusivity with miracles, except that it does not indicate superiority (al-afdaliyya) in the sense of increase in reward and honor with Allah after the establishment of the agreement that is tantamount to consensus (al-ijma') on the superiority of Abu Bakr, then 'Umar. And Ali's acknowledgment of that. Moreover, in what was mentioned there are points of investigation not hidden from the one who investigates (al-muhassil), such as: that by 'ourselves' is meant the soul of the Prophet (peace be upon him), as is said: I called myself to such and such; and that the obligation of love and the establishment of victory, assuming they are realized in the case of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) are not exclusive to him. Likewise the perfections established for the mentioned prophets. And that 'the most beloved of your creation' may be interpreted as specifically including Abu Bakr and 'Umar, in accordance with the proofs of their superiority, and it may be intended as 'the most beloved of the creation to you' so that he eats from it. And that the ruling of brotherhood is established in the case of Abu Bakr and 'Uthman (may Allah be pleased with them both) as well, where he said regarding Abu Bakr: 'But he is my brother, my companion, and my minister,' and he said regarding 'Uthman: 'My brother and my companion in Paradise.' As for the hadith of knowledge and courage, no incident occurred but Abu Bakr and 'Umar had an opinion in it, and at the time of disagreement, his opinion was never referred to at all, but rather [something omitted]. And firmness of composure and courage of heart and disregard for perils was not less in Abu Bakr than in anyone, especially in the incidents that occurred after the Prophet (peace be upon him) that almost caused a weakness in Islam. And goodness is not in guiding from [the story of] al-Khazirah, then he had not finished cooking it when a poor person came, and they gave him food; then he made the second third, and when it was cooked an orphan came and they fed him; then he made the remaining third, and when it was done cooking a captive from the polytheists came and they fed him, and they went hungry that day, so these verses were revealed.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a response (jawab) to the claim that Ali's extensive merits prove his absolute superiority (afdaliyya) over Abu Bakr and Umar. The author acknowledges all of Ali's merits but denies that they entail superiority in reward and honor with Allah. The primary reason is that there is an agreement that amounts to consensus (ijma') on the superiority of Abu Bakr, then Umar, and Ali himself acknowledged this. The author then points out several interpretive nuances (mawadi' bahth) in the evidence cited for Ali's superiority:
1. The phrase 'ourselves' (anfusana) in the hadith of brotherhood refers to the Prophet himself, not to Ali exclusively.
2. The obligation of love and victory is not exclusive to Ali.
3. The attribute 'most beloved of creation' can be interpreted as referring to Abu Bakr and Umar based on proofs of their superiority.
4. The brotherhood mentioned in hadiths also applies to Abu Bakr and Uthman, not only Ali.
5. Regarding knowledge and courage, in every incident Abu Bakr and Umar had opinions, and when there was disagreement, Ali's opinion was not followed at all (the phrase 'بل قد وقد' suggests something is omitted but implies that their views prevailed).
6. Abu Bakr's courage and composure were not less than anyone's, especially in critical post-Prophetic events.
7. Finally, the story of al-Khazirah (a dish) reveals that the verse about feeding the poor, orphan, and captive was revealed concerning Ali and his family, but this does not prove superiority because the act of feeding was done out of piety, not as a claim to rank. The response systematically undermines the arguments that would use these merits to elevate Ali above Abu Bakr and Umar.

Key Terms
أفضلية
إجماع
محصل
أخوة
خزيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A long list of personal virtues automatically proves a person is spiritually superior to others, regardless of other evidence.
Correction: Virtues must be interpreted in light of consensus (ijma) and other proofs; they do not by themselves establish absolute superiority.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the main reason that Ali's merits do not prove his superiority over Abu Bakr and Umar?
A) Because Ali himself denied any superiority
B) Because there is an agreement tantamount to consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar
C) Because Ali's merits are not authentic
D) Because Abu Bakr and Umar have more enumerated merits

Key Takeaway
Personal merits, even when abundant, do not by themselves establish absolute superiority; they must be assessed within the broader framework of consensus (ijma) and sound interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you admire someone for their virtues, do you ever stop to ask whether your admiration is based on the full picture or only on selected merits? How can you ensure your judgments about people's spiritual rank are not biased by partial evidence?
?
This lesson shows that even clear merits like those of Ali must be interpreted within consensus and other evidences. Is there a person in your life you have judged as 'better' or 'lesser' based on incomplete information? What would it take for you to reevaluate that judgment?
?
The author points out that Ali himself acknowledged the superiority of Abu Bakr and Umar. Reflect on your own humility: are you willing to accept the collective wisdom of the community (ijma) even when it seems to contradict your personal inclinations? What is one area where your opinion might need to yield to the consensus of scholars?

Ali's Virtues and the Consensus on Superiority
Acknowledges virtues then rejects superiority via consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ali's abundant virtues do not automatically prove his superiority over Abu Bakr and Umar according to Sunni consensus.

Core Idea
Virtues do not outweigh consensus and Ali's own acknowledgment.

The author begins by acknowledging Ali's numerous virtues—forbearance, eloquence, and early conversion. Yet he immediately counters that these do not imply superiority in reward with Allah because there is a consensus-like agreement on the preference of Abu Bakr then Umar, and Ali himself recognized that.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening virtues
Note the three examples of Ali's virtues: forbearance (even toward Ibn Muljam), eloquence (Nahj al-Balagha), and early Islam (Tuesday after Monday mission).
The author concedes these are genuine.
2
Understand the main answer
The author states that despite these virtues, they do not prove 'afdaliyya' (superiority in reward) because there is an agreement amounting to consensus (ijma') on the superiority of Abu Bakr then Umar, and Ali himself confirmed this.
This is the core refutation.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice that the author first lists evidence for the claim, then gives a principled answer that transcends the particulars. This is a standard kalam method.

الإسلام وتكثير أمة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : وأما حديث زهدهما في الدنيا، فغني عن البيان. وأما السابق إسلاماً، فقيل: علي. وقيل : زيد بن حارثة وقيل : خديجة . وقيل : أبو بكر، وعليه الأكثرون ، على ما أمراً على الشأن جلي البرهان غني عن البيان
And he was the most forbearing of them, to the point that he left Ibn Muljam in his home and neighborhood, giving him gifts, knowing his condition, and he pardoned Marwan when he showed intense enmity on the Day of the Camel, and his statement about him: 'The nation will meet from him and his offspring a red day.' Also, he was the most eloquent of them in tongue, as witnessed by the book Nahj al-Balagha, and the earliest of them in Islam, as it is narrated that the Prophet was sent on Monday and 'Ali embraced Islam on Tuesday. In short, his virtues are too apparent to be hidden and too numerous to be counted. The answer: there is no dispute about the generality of his virtues, his abundant excellences, his possession of perfections, and his specialization in miracles, except that this does not indicate preference in the sense of increased reward and honor with God, after the establishment of the agreement that runs in the course of consensus on the preference of Abu Bakr, then 'Umar. And the acknowledgment by 'Ali of that. Moreover, in what was mentioned there are points of discussion not hidden from the one who has obtained (knowledge), such as: that the meaning of 'our selves' is the self of the Prophet (peace be upon him), as it is said: I called myself to such-and-such; and that the obligation of love and the confirmation of support, assuming its realization in the right of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him), is not specific to him; likewise the perfections established for the mentioned ones from among the prophets; and that 'the most beloved of Your creation' may be understood as specific to Abu Bakr and 'Umar from it, acting on the evidence of their preference, and it may be interpreted as intending 'the most beloved of creation to You' in terms of eating from it. And that the ruling of brotherhood is established in the right of Abu Bakr and 'Uthman (may God be pleased with them both) as well, where he said regarding Abu Bakr: 'But he is my brother, my companion, and my minister,' and he said regarding 'Uthman: 'My brother and my companion in Paradise.' As for the hadith of knowledge and courage, no event occurred but that Abu Bakr and 'Umar had an opinion in it, and at the time of disagreement, the opinion of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) was never referred to at all, but rather... And self-possession, courage of heart, and disregard for dangers were not less in Abu Bakr than in anyone, especially in the events that occurred after the Prophet (peace be upon him) that nearly caused weakness in Islam. And the good is not less than the guidance of one from the stew; when its cooking was completed, a poor person came and they brought food to him, then they prepared the second third, and when its cooking was completed, an orphan came and asked, so they fed him, then they prepared the remaining third, and when its cooking was completed, a captive from the polytheists came and they fed him, and they went without that day, and so these verses were revealed about it. Guidance by the blessing of Abu Bakr, his auspicious invitation, and his good management is not less than the good in killing those whom 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) killed of the disbelievers; rather, perhaps that is more effective in supporting Islam and increasing the nation of the Prophet (peace be upon him). As for the hadith of their asceticism in this world, it is needless of explanation. As for the precedence in Islam, it is said: 'Ali; and it is said: Zayd ibn Haritha; and it is said: Khadija; and it is said: Abu Bakr, and the majority are upon this, as was declared by Hassan ibn Thabit in a poem that he recited before witnesses, and no one denied it. And it is said: the first to believe in him among women was Khadija (may God be pleased with her), among boys was 'Ali (may God be pleased with him), among slaves was Zayd ibn Haritha, and among free men was Abu Bakr (may God be pleased with him), and a group of great men followed him, such as 'Uthman, al-Zubayr, Talha, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Abu 'Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah, and others. And fairness is that the efforts of Abu Bakr and 'Umar in Islam are a matter of great importance, clear proof, needless of explanation.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that 'Ali's virtues prove his superiority over Abu Bakr and 'Umar. The author first lists some of 'Ali's virtues: his forbearance (even toward Ibn Muljam and Marwan), his eloquence (as per Nahj al-Balagha), and his early conversion to Islam (on Tuesday after the Prophet's mission began on Monday). Then the author states the answer: although there is no doubt about 'Ali's many virtues, they do not indicate preference in terms of reward with God, because there is consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr then 'Umar, and 'Ali himself acknowledged this. The author then points out problems in the specific arguments: the phrase 'our selves' refers to the Prophet himself, not 'Ali; the obligation of love and support is not exclusive to 'Ali; the hadith 'the most beloved of Your creation' could be interpreted as applying to Abu Bakr and 'Umar; the brotherhood with the Prophet is also affirmed for Abu Bakr and 'Uthman; and as for knowledge and courage, Abu Bakr and 'Umar had opinions in every incident, and no one turned to 'Ali's view. Then the author mentions a story about feeding the poor, orphan, and captive, which is a virtue of 'Ali and Fatima, but compares it to Abu Bakr's guidance, arguing that the good from Abu Bakr's efforts is not less than 'Ali's killing of disbelievers. The author then discusses asceticism, stating it is self-evident, and addresses the issue of precedence in Islam, presenting various opinions before concluding that the majority hold that Abu Bakr was the first free man to embrace Islam, and that many great companions followed him. The passage ends with a declaration that the efforts of Abu Bakr and 'Umar in Islam are clear and need no explanation.

Key Terms
أفضلية
إجماع
مناقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That possessing many virtues automatically makes one superior in religious rank.
Correction: Superiority (afdaliyya) is determined by divine reward, which is established by consensus and acknowledgment of the individuals themselves, not merely by enumeration of merits.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main reason for denying that Ali's virtues prove his superiority?
A) Ali's virtues are not genuine.
B) There is an agreement amounting to consensus on the preference of Abu Bakr then Umar, and Ali acknowledged that.
C) Ali's virtues are fewer than those of Abu Bakr and Umar.
D) The Prophet explicitly denied Ali's superiority.

Key Takeaway
Virtues alone do not establish superiority in rank; consensus and the subject's own testimony carry decisive weight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What virtues or achievements do you secretly believe make you 'better' than others, and when was the last time you honestly acknowledged someone else's superiority in a domain you care about?
?
If you were in 'Ali's position—having undeniable virtues but being asked to accept a consensus that places others above you—would your ego let you submit? What would it cost you to say, 'They are better'?
?
Is there a person or group you have refused to acknowledge as more deserving, even though deep down you know the evidence supports it? What fear or pride is stopping you from releasing your claim to superiority?

Rebuttal of Specific Proof-Texts
Refutes specific textual arguments for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes several specific textual arguments used to claim Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Texts cited for Ali's superiority are either not exclusive or interpretable differently.

After the overarching answer, the author addresses particular proof-texts: the phrase 'our selves', the hadith of 'most beloved of creation', the brotherhood with the Prophet, and claims of knowledge and courage. He shows that these either apply to others as well or are not decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttals carefully
The author discusses: 1) 'our selves' refers to the Prophet himself, 2) obligation of love and support is not specific to Ali, 3) 'most beloved' may refer to Abu Bakr and Umar, 4) brotherhood is also affirmed for Abu Bakr and Uthman.
Each point is a separate refutation.
2
Note the method
The author uses alternative interpretations and parallel texts to weaken the exclusive claim. This shows how kalam handles contested evidence.
3
Assess the strength
Consider how each rebuttal fits with the overall consensus argument. They are supplementary but not strictly necessary.

How does the author rebut the claim that 'our selves' in the mubahala verse refers to Ali?
A) He denies the authenticity of the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read hadiths praising a specific Companion, do you instinctively assume it proves their superiority over others, or do you pause to consider how the text might be interpreted in light of other evidence? This lesson challenges you to examine your own tendencies toward partiality.
?
If you have ever held a belief about the superiority of a particular Companion based on a single text, would you be willing to reconsider it after seeing the alternative interpretations provided here? What would it cost you to subject your cherished views to the same scrutiny?
?
The author argues that brotherhood, love, and knowledge are not exclusive to Ali. How does your heart react to the possibility that your loyalty to a person or group may have led you to overlook what the broader tradition affirms? Will you actively seek to correct that imbalance today?

Comparing Virtues: Actions and Precedence
Compares specific virtues and discusses precedence in Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author compares Ali's concrete acts (like feeding the poor) with the broader guidance and efforts of Abu Bakr and Umar, and addresses the issue of precedence in Islam.

Core Idea
The guidance of Abu Bakr outweighs single acts of virtue, and precedence in Islam is disputed.

The author continues by comparing a famous story of Ali and Fatima feeding the poor, orphan, and captive with the guidance and good management of Abu Bakr, arguing that the latter is not less beneficial than Ali's killing of disbelievers. Then he discusses the topic of who was first to embrace Islam, presenting multiple opinions before concluding that the majority say Abu Bakr was the first free man.

Guided Path
1
Read the comparison story
The author recounts a story where Ali and Fatima cooked food and gave it away to a poor person, an orphan, and a captive, going without food themselves. This is a virtue of Ali.
The story is well-known.
2
Understand the counterpoint
The author then argues that the guidance (hidaya) brought about by Abu Bakr's efforts is not less than the good in Ali's killing of disbelievers; it may even be more beneficial for Islam.
This reframes the comparison.
3
Analyze the precedence discussion
The author lists various claims about who was first to accept Islam: Ali, Zayd, Khadija, Abu Bakr. He notes that the majority (including a poem by Hassan ibn Thabit) affirm Abu Bakr as the first free man, and that many great companions followed him.
This is a factual debate with multiple views.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The passage ends by asserting that the efforts of Abu Bakr and Umar in Islam are 'of great importance, clear proof, needless of explanation.'
This ties back to the overall argument.

And he was the most forbearing of them, to the point that he left Ibn Muljam in his home and neighborhood, giving him gifts, knowing his condition, and he pardoned Marwan when he showed intense enmity on the Day of the Camel, and his statement about him: 'The nation will meet from him and his offspring a red day.' In short, his virtues are too apparent to be hidden and too numerous to be counted. The answer: there is no dispute about the generality of his virtues, his abundant excellences, his possession of perfections, and his specialization in miracles, except that this does not indicate preference in the sense of increased reward and honor with God, after the establishment of the agreement that runs in the course of consensus on the preference of Abu Bakr, then 'Umar. And the acknowledgment by 'Ali of that. Moreover, in what was mentioned there are points of discussion not hidden from the one who has obtained (knowledge), such as: that the meaning of 'our selves' is the self of the Prophet (peace be upon him), as it is said: I called myself to such-and-such; and that the obligation of love and the confirmation of support, assuming its realization in the right of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him), is not specific to him; likewise the perfections established for the mentioned ones from among the prophets; and that 'the most beloved of Your creation' may be understood as specific to Abu Bakr and 'Umar from it, acting on the evidence of their preference, and it may be interpreted as intending 'the most beloved of creation to You' in terms of eating from it. And that the ruling of brotherhood is established in the right of Abu Bakr and 'Uthman (may God be pleased with them both) as well, where he said regarding Abu Bakr: 'But he is my brother, my companion, and my minister,' and he said regarding 'Uthman: 'My brother and my companion in Paradise.' As for the hadith of knowledge and courage, no event occurred but that Abu Bakr and 'Umar had an opinion in it, and at the time of disagreement, the opinion of 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) was never referred to at all, but rather... And self-possession, courage of heart, and disregard for dangers were not less in Abu Bakr than in anyone, especially in the events that occurred after the Prophet (peace be upon him) that nearly caused weakness in Islam. And the good is not less than the guidance of one from the stew; when its cooking was completed, a poor person came and they brought food to him, then they prepared the second third, and when its cooking was completed, an orphan came and asked, so they fed him, then they prepared the remaining third, and when its cooking was completed, a captive from the polytheists came and they fed him, and they went without that day, and so these verses were revealed about it. Guidance by the blessing of Abu Bakr, his auspicious invitation, and his good management is not less than the good in killing those whom 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) killed of the disbelievers; rather, perhaps that is more effective in supporting Islam and increasing the nation of the Prophet (peace be upon him). As for the hadith of their asceticism in this world, it is needless of explanation. As for the precedence in Islam, it is said: 'Ali; and it is said: Zayd ibn Haritha; and it is said: Khadija; and it is said: Abu Bakr, and the majority are upon this, as was declared by Hassan ibn Thabit in a poem that he recited before witnesses, and no one denied it. And it is said: the first to believe in him among women was Khadija (may God be pleased with her), among boys was 'Ali (may God be pleased with him), among slaves was Zayd ibn Haritha, and among free men was Abu Bakr (may God be pleased with him), and a group of great men followed him, such as 'Uthman, al-Zubayr, Talha, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Abu 'Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah, and others. And fairness is that the efforts of Abu Bakr and 'Umar in Islam are a matter of great importance, clear proof, needless of explanation.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that 'Ali's virtues prove his superiority over Abu Bakr and 'Umar. The author first lists some of 'Ali's virtues: his forbearance (even toward Ibn Muljam and Marwan), his eloquence (as per Nahj al-Balagha), and his early conversion to Islam (on Tuesday after the Prophet's mission began on Monday). Then the author states the answer: although there is no doubt about 'Ali's many virtues, they do not indicate preference in terms of reward with God, because there is consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr then 'Umar, and 'Ali himself acknowledged this. The author then points out problems in the specific arguments: the phrase 'our selves' refers to the Prophet himself, not 'Ali; the obligation of love and support is not exclusive to 'Ali; the hadith 'the most beloved of Your creation' could be interpreted as applying to Abu Bakr and 'Umar; the brotherhood with the Prophet is also affirmed for Abu Bakr and 'Uthman; and as for knowledge and courage, Abu Bakr and 'Umar had opinions in every incident, and no one turned to 'Ali's view. Then the author mentions a story about feeding the poor, orphan, and captive, which is a virtue of 'Ali and Fatima, but compares it to Abu Bakr's guidance, arguing that the good from Abu Bakr's efforts is not less than 'Ali's killing of disbelievers. The author then discusses asceticism, stating it is self-evident, and addresses the issue of precedence in Islam, presenting various opinions before concluding that the majority hold that Abu Bakr was the first free man to embrace Islam, and that many great companions followed him. The passage ends with a declaration that the efforts of Abu Bakr and 'Umar in Islam are clear and need no explanation.

Key Terms
السابق إسلاماً
هداية
بركة
نصرة الإسلام

Checkpoint

C) Both are equal.

Key Takeaway
When comparing virtues, one must consider the overall impact on the Muslim community, not just single acts.
